    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="6016fe8416a04c01b6f4dd992ba61933">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '6d6bd894-2c87-4a22-824f-b0047f6290aa'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d126473064" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>A NASICON-type Mn/F dual-doping Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> cathode material is successfully synthesized via a spray drying method. A medium-spin of Fe is measured by DFT calculation, X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES), temperature-dependent magnetization susceptibility (M−T) measurement, and electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) tests. It indicates that the <i>e</i><sub>g</sub> orbital occupation of Fe<sup>2+</sup> can be finely regulated, thus optimizing the bond strength between the oxidation and reduction processes. Furthermore, from UV−vis DRS and four-point probe conductivity measurements, it can be seen that, after adjusting the electron spin states, the band gap of the material has decreased from 1.01 to 0.80&nbsp;eV, and the electronic conductivity has increased from 8.5 to 24.4 µS cm<sup>−1</sup>, thereby leading to competitive electrochemical performance. The as-optimized Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> displays both excellent rate performance (121.0 and 104.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and 5 C, respectively) and outstanding cycling stability (88.5% capacity retention after 1000 cycles at 1 C). The results indicate that this low-cost Mn/F dual-doping Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> cathode can be a competitive candidate material for sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202309701-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202309701-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Over the past few years, there has been significant progress in large-scale electric energy storage technologies due to the growing demand for efficient utilization of renewable energy resources. Among the various solutions, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as promising candidates for energy storage, primarily because of the abundant and low-cost availability of sodium resources. However, one of the major challenges in the development of SIBs is the need for more suitable cathode electrodes that meet the requirements of high energy density and cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0001_R_d126472933e685" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Environ. Sci.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Numerous cathode materials have been explored for SIBs, including polyanion compounds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0002_R_d126472933e692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Carbon Energy">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> transition-metal oxides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0003_R_d126472933e697" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Electrochem. Energy R.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Prussian blue analogs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0004_R_d126472933e702" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Electrochem. Energy R.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, the sodium superionic conductor (NASICON) type structure iron-based phosphate (Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, referred to as NFPP) has garnered significant attention as a promising candidate. NFPP boasts a 3D framework with excellent structural stability and strong covalent bonding, making it attractive for practical applications due to its appropriate theoretical capacity (129 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), environmental-friendliness, and low volume change during charging and discharging processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0005_R_d126472933e723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the poor intrinsic electronic conductivity of NFPP (ranging from 10<sup>−12</sup>–10<sup>−8</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>) has hindered large-scale practical applications. Several methods have been attempted to enhance the electronic conductivity of NASICON materials.</p>
         
         <p>In 2012, Kang<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0006_R_d126472933e738" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> et&nbsp;al. introduced a new Fe-based mixed phosphate-pyrophosphate NASICON type Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) cathode material for SIBs, which could deliver a discharge capacity of 113.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1C. This novel cathode material exhibited a higher discharge voltage (≈3.1&nbsp;V) compared to other phosphate cathode materials, e.g., 3.0&nbsp;V for Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0007_R_d126472933e765" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 2.7&nbsp;V for NaFePO<sub>4</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0008_R_d126472933e772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Energy">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and 2.4&nbsp;V for FePO<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0009_R_d126472933e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In 2020, Wang<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0010_R_d126472933e785" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Small">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> et&nbsp;al. demonstrated that anion SiO<sub>4</sub><sup>4−</sup> doping can reduce the bandgap of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> lattice and enhance its electronic conductivity, structural stability, and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. They found that the improved Na<sub>3.1</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2.9</sub>(SiO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>0.1</sub> could deliver a high capacity of 82.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at 20 C and excellent cyclic stability (≈98% capacity retention after 500 cycles at 1 C). Similarly, in 2022, Li<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0011_R_d126472933e820" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Power Sources Energy Storage Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> et&nbsp;al. showed that metal cation (Mn<sup>2+</sup>) doping can enhance the intrinsic electronic conductivity and structural stability of the material, resulting in a significant initial discharge capacity of 131.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, a superior rate performance (70.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 C) and long cycling stability.</p>
         
         <p>Despite these creative developments, each individual strategy only led to limited improvements in the electrochemical behavior of the electrode. Therefore, the authors propose a novel approach that combines the functional combination of metal cation and anion dual-doping to create a modified NFPP.</p>
         
         <p>Hence, a cathode material (Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, denoted as NFPP) for SIBs was prepared by a dual-doping strategy of anions (F) and cations (Mn) through spray drying combined with a high-temperature calcination method (denoted as NFPP-Mn-F). The careful design of Mn/F dual-doping adjusts the electron spin of Fe<sup>2+</sup> and enhances electronic transport, resulting in excellent electrochemical kinetics. As expected, the NFPP-Mn-F electrode demonstrates remarkable potential with a large specific capacity and exceptional cycling longevity.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202309701-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202309701-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The synthetic procedure of the NFPP-Mn-F was schematically described in <b>Scheme</b> <a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0006">1</a>. Briefly, a certain proportion of Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O, citric acid, NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, manganic salt, and fluoride salt were dissolved in deionized water (process I), then conducted spray drying to obtain dried precursor powder (process II), and finally calcined it at high temperature under Ar/H<sub>2</sub> gas (process III) to obtain the final required NFPPx cathode materials. More experimental details were provided in the “Experimental” section.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202309701-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/929de933-d8d4-45ee-b293-a8fcb357a239/adfm202309701-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/929de933-d8d4-45ee-b293-a8fcb357a239/adfm202309701-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f38d6eaf-8e36-468b-8b70-4bdd8a82b449/adfm202309701-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/929de933-d8d4-45ee-b293-a8fcb357a239/adfm202309701-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202309701-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202309701" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the formation process of NFPPx cathode materials.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202309701-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202309701-sec-0030-title">2.1 Materials Characterizations</h3>
            
            <p>To obtain accurate phase information, X-ray diffraction (XRD) was utilized to determine the phase constitution and atomic site occupations of the NFPP samples. The XRD results indicate that all NFPP samples (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0001">1a</a>) are similar and match well with the previously reported NFPP,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0006_R_d126472933e912" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> demonstrating that a small amount of doped Mn<sup>2+</sup> or F<sup>−</sup> does not alter the NFPP crystal structure. However, some diffraction peaks, such as those displayed in the magnified spectra ((411) and (222) peaks), exhibit slight shifts to lower angles after Mn<sup>2+</sup> or F<sup>−</sup> doping (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0001">1a</a> enlarged patterns). This shift indicates an increase in the corresponding lattice cell, which enhances the kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> during cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0012_R_d126472933e932" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202309701-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ce0253f7-6564-43fc-a34a-afe1f65dbc38/adfm202309701-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ce0253f7-6564-43fc-a34a-afe1f65dbc38/adfm202309701-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8cb78ee4-0af7-4b46-8a6c-584a50969d44/adfm202309701-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ce0253f7-6564-43fc-a34a-afe1f65dbc38/adfm202309701-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202309701-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202309701" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterizations of the as-prepared samples. a) XRD patterns and enlarged patterns (left) from 25° to 26.5°, (right) from 30° to 35.2°. b) Rietveld refinements of NFPP-Mn-F. The insets are schematic representations of the refinement results. c) SEM image and EDS mapping results for selected elements of NFPP-Mn-F. d) TEM image and inverse FFT of NFPP-Mn-F particle.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The slight shrinkage in the NFPP-Mn lattice cell can be attributed to the larger ionic radius of Mn<sup>2+</sup> (0.67 Å) compared to Fe<sup>2+</sup> (0.61 Å), confirming the successful incorporation of Mn dopants into the crystal structure of NFPP.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0011_R_d126472933e962" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Power Sources Energy Storage Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0013_R_d126472933e962" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater.2022">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, the results for NFPP-F may seem to contradict Vegard's rule due to the slightly smaller ionic radius of fluorine (1.33 Å) compared to oxygen (1.40 Å). However, this is consistent with the formation of the larger Fe<sup>2+</sup> (0.61 Å) cation from Fe<sup>3+</sup> (0.55 Å) during fluorination,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0014_R_d126472933e972" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0015_R_d126472933e972" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Phys. D: Appl. Phys.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as confirmed by the Fe 2p level shown in X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectra. The proportion of Fe<sup>2+</sup> cations in the as-prepared NFPP, NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F, and NFPP-Mn-F samples was found to be 81.6%, 82.3%, 86.2%, and 86.8%, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2d</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0001">1b</a> displays the successful Rietveld refinement of the NFPP-Mn-F material, showing a favorable weighted profile <i>R</i> factor (<i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 11.43%). The NFPP-Mn-F material was indexed to the orthorhombic Pn21a space group, with lattice parameters <i>a</i> = 18.0614 Å, <i>b</i> = 6.5317 Å, <i>c</i> = 10.6402 Å, and <i>V</i> = 1255.2419 Å<sup>3</sup>, as Kang's group previously reported.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0006_R_d126472933e1004" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The typical crystalline structure is depicted in the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0001">1b</a>. In the NFPP-Mn-F, the [Fe<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>13</sub>] units, composed of three FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and three PO<sub>4</sub> groups, constitute a 3D framework with extensive ionic channels. This structure enables rapid sodium-ion diffusion. The incorporated Mn is presumed to occupy the Fe site, while F fills the O site, a result of the similar radii of Mn<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup>, as well as F<sup>−</sup> and O<sup>2−</sup>. To further examine differences in crystal structure, Rietveld refinements of XRD patterns of NFPP, NFPP-Mn, and NFPP-F were executed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The refinement outcomes (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) showed that the lattice parameters of NFPP increase to varying degrees following the doping of Mn<sup>2+</sup> or F<sup>−</sup>, facilitating Na-ion diffusion. The chemical compositions of the prepared samples were further characterized using XPS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The presence of Mn (or F) was detected in NFPP-Mn (or NFPP-F) and NFPP-Mn-F, reinforcing that Mn<sup>2+</sup> (or F<sup>−</sup>) was successfully incorporated in the samples mentioned.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0011_R_d126472933e1050" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Power Sources Energy Storage Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0016_R_d126472933e1050" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Radioanal. Nucl. Ch.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The morphology and element distribution of the prepared NFPP, NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F, and NFPP-Mn-F samples were investigated using scanning electron microscopy (SEM), energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS), and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) technologies (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0001">1c,d</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3,S4</a>, Supporting Information). The SEM results showed all four samples are microspheres with a particle size of ≈5&nbsp;µm. This suggests that the doping of elements (Mn, F, and Mn/F dual-doping) does not significantly alter the morphology of the prepared materials. Furthermore, the EDS elemental mappings (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0001">1c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) indicated the uniform distribution of Na, Mn, Fe, P, O, and F in the samples, suggesting that the doping elements were incorporated into the NFPP crystal instead of segregating to MnO or other Mn species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0013_R_d126472933e1069" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater.2022">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>A thin C-coating layer, measuring between 1–3&nbsp;nm in thickness, was observed on the surface of the NFPP-Mn-F nanoparticle in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0001">1d</a>. Moreover, the specific carbon content of the four materials was measured by TG and DSC, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information). The elementary analysis shows that the percentages of carbon content in NFPP, NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F, and NFPP-Mn-F are 10.3%, 10.0%, 9.3%, and 10.3%, respectively. This also revealed lattice fringes with a spacing of ≈0.3542&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the (411) crystalline plane of the NFPP-Mn-F. The interplanar spacings of the (411) plane for NFPP, NFPP-Mn, and NFPP-F samples were 0.3471, 0.3520, and 0.3482&nbsp;nm, respectively, as displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). This suggests that the doping of elements (Mn, F, and Mn/F dual-doping) can expand the interplanar spacing of NFPP,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0017_R_d126472933e1087" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a>, <a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0018_R_d126472933e1087" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Electrochem. Commun.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> aligning well with the Rietveld refinements of XRD patterns. The increased interplanar spacing enhances the kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> during cycling, suggesting the potential for improved electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0012_R_d126472933e1095" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>To further elucidate the local electronic state, techniques such as synchrotron X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES), extended X-ray absorption fine structure spectroscopy, UV−vis diffuse reflectance spectra (UV−vis DRS), temperature-dependent magnetization susceptibility (M-T) measurement, electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) spectroscopy, and four-point probe conductivity measurement were employed. As displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0002">2a</a>, the absorption edge of NFPP-F and NFPP-Mn-F samples shifted toward lower energy (Fe foil), suggesting that F doping reduces the valence state of the Fe species in the NFPP-F and NFPP-Mn-F samples. This finding was also corroborated by the XPS spectrum in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202309701-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d466a5d3-696d-45cb-bc66-57eb15f74811/adfm202309701-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d466a5d3-696d-45cb-bc66-57eb15f74811/adfm202309701-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9c4333df-a01a-4e0b-92b3-1c3901df04b7/adfm202309701-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d466a5d3-696d-45cb-bc66-57eb15f74811/adfm202309701-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202309701-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202309701" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electronic structure of Fe. a) Fe K-edge XANES, b) Fourier-transformed, c) M-T measurement, d) EPR spectra with g-factor of the NFPP, NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F and NFPP-Mn-F samples. e) Schematic representation of the spin transition of Fe, f) UV−vis DRS, g) four-point probe conductivity measurement of the NFPP, NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F, and NFPP-Mn-F.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Further structural details were inferred from the Fourier-transformed extended X-ray absorption fine structure spectra of the Fe K-edge (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0002">2b</a>). The plots for the four samples showed two main peaks centered ≈ 1.6 and 2.5 Å in R-space, corresponding to the Fe─O and Fe─Fe peak positions, respectively. Remarkably, F doping caused a shift in the Fe─O peak position from 1.62 Å (NFPP) to 1.56 Å (NFPP-F), while Mn doping made virtually no difference. In contrast, with the Fe─Fe peak position, F doping had no effect, whereas the NFPP-Mn shifted from 2.54 Å (NFPP) to 2.57 Å (NFPP-Mn). Interestingly, after Mn-F dual-doping, the Fe─O and Fe─Fe bonds of NFPP-Mn-F shifted from 1.62 Å (NFPP) to 1.59 Å (NFPP-Mn-F) and 2.54 Å (NFPP) to 2.51 Å (NFPP-Mn-F), respectively. In other words, only in the NFPP-Mn-F sample did both the Fe─O and Fe─Fe bonds concurrently shorten, potentially enhancing electron transfer during the charge-discharge process<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0019_R_d126472933e1136" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nano Energy">19</a>, <a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0020_R_d126472933e1136" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Alloy. Compd.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The M-T test, performed under a magnetic field of H = 1 kOe (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0002">2c</a>), revealed a decreased slope with the introduction of Mn or F doping. This suggests an enhanced paramagnetic state of the samples, indicating fewer free electrons with Pauli paramagnetism circulating around the Fe center, in alignment with the XPS results. EPR (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0002">2d</a>) was also conducted to reveal the electron spin configuration of the four prepared samples. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0002">2d</a>, the EPR spectra presented significant shifts after element doping (Mn, F, or Mn/F dual-doping). The differences in the g factor between the doped and bare samples were Δ<i>g</i>2 = 0.7E-4 (NFPP-Mn), Δ<i>g</i>3 = 4.8E-4 (NFPP-F), and Δ<i>g</i>4 = 3.0E-4 (NFPP-Mn-F). It is widely accepted that an unpaired electron that gains or loses angular momentum can modify the value of its g factor.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0022_R_d126472933e1159" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interestingly, NFPP-F showed the largest change, NFPP-Mn-F showed a moderate change, while NFPP-Mn displayed a slight change, suggesting the 3d electron-spin of Fe in the NFPP-F sample is likely high-spin (HS: t<sub>2g</sub><sup>4</sup>e<sub>g</sub><sup>2</sup>), NFPP-Mn-F medium-spin (MS: t<sub>2g</sub><sup>5</sup>e<sub>g</sub><sup>1</sup>), and NFPP-Mn low-spin (LS: t<sub>2g</sub><sup>6</sup>e<sub>g</sub><sup>0</sup>). High-spin, intermediate, and low-spin Fe possess different electronic configurations of d<sub>x</sub><sup>2</sup><sub>-y</sub><sup>2</sup>d<sub>z</sub><sup>2</sup>d<sub>xy</sub>d<sub>xz</sub>d<sub>yz</sub>, d<sub>x</sub><sup>2</sup><sub>-y</sub><sup>2</sup>d<sub>xy</sub>d<sub>xz</sub>d<sub>yz,</sub> and d<sub>xy</sub>d<sub>yz</sub>d<sub>xz</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0002">2e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0023_R_d126472933e1221" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Appl. Catal. B-Environ.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Indeed, an optimal electrophilicity of the active dual-doping NFPP-Mn-F leads to a bond strength between the oxidation and reduction processes that is neither too weak nor too strong, reducing the electronic conductivity of cathodes and thus enhancing the electrochemical performance of materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0021_R_d126472933e1226" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Energy Lett.">21</a>, <a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0024_R_d126472933e1226" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Catal.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The above predictions were validated through UV−vis DRS and four-point probe conductivity measurements to investigate the band gap and electron conductivity of the prepared samples. As calculated from the Tauc plots, the optical band gap (including carbon layer) of NFPP, NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F, and NFPP-Mn-F samples was ≈1.01, 0.96, 0.88, and 0.8&nbsp;eV, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0021_R_d126472933e1233" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Energy Lett.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These findings suggest that Mn or F doping can reduce the band gap of NFPP, thereby reducing electronic transition resistance and enhancing the electrochemical performance of the prepared cathodes. To obtain a clearer understanding of the material's electronic conductivity, a four-point probe conductivity measurement was performed. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0002">2g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information) reveal that the average conductivities of the NFPP, NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F, and NFPP-Mn-F were 8.5, 13.5, 17.9, and 24.4 µS cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. This aligns with the previous prediction in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0002">2e</a>, which stated that the addition of doping elements (Mn, F, and Mn/F) significantly enhances the sample's conductivity, especially in the case of Mn/F dual-doping. Hence, we can further predict that the electrochemical performance of the material will improve with the addition of doped elements.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202309701-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202309701-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Properties</h3>
            
            <p>The charge/discharge profiles of the samples prepared at a current density of 0.1C (where 1 C = 129 mAg<sup>−1</sup>, with a voltage range of 1.8-4.2&nbsp;V, at 30 °C) are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0003">3a</a>. The specific discharge capacities of the NFPP, NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F, and NFPP-Mn-F electrodes reached 117.3, 114.3, 112.2, and 121.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, with coulombic efficiencies of 98.2%, 97.1%, 98.7%, and 97.5%. This data indicates that the doping of single elements (either Mn<sup>2+</sup> or F<sup>−</sup>) resulted in a decrease in specific discharge capacity, potentially due to the occupation of active element (Fe) sites by inactive elements (especially Mn), which leads to a reduction in specific capacities. However, the specific capacity slightly increased from 117.3 to 121.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with the dual-doping of cation and anion elements, illustrating the effectiveness of this strategy in improving electrochemical performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202309701-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4f4a6d1f-913d-4f33-8afb-aa54b10016bd/adfm202309701-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4f4a6d1f-913d-4f33-8afb-aa54b10016bd/adfm202309701-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d0587682-548c-4573-b7f1-10f6de7a8183/adfm202309701-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4f4a6d1f-913d-4f33-8afb-aa54b10016bd/adfm202309701-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202309701-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202309701" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical profiles of the as-prepared samples. a) The charge-discharge profiles at 0.1C. b) <i>dQ</i>/<i>dV</i> curves. c) The charge-discharge profiles from 0.1C to 5C. d) The rate performance. e) Cycling performance at 1C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0003">3b</a> presents the <i>dQ/dV</i> curves of all the electrodes. Along with two pairs of well-defined redox peaks appearing at 2.93/2.90 and 3.23/3.22&nbsp;V, two pairs of weak redox peaks emerged at 3.19/3.17 and 3.13/3.10&nbsp;V. These represent the Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> redox couple when sodium ion insertion/extraction occurs at different sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0025_R_d126472933e1312" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Storage Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interestingly, several distinct and sharper peaks (3.23/3.22, 3.19/3.17, and 2.93/2.90&nbsp;V) were present in the NFPP-Mn-F electrode profile. These redox couples will represent outstanding reversibility in subsequent experiments.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0026_R_d126472933e1318" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Storage Mater. Chem. Eng. J.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The charge-discharge profiles of the NFPP-Mn-F electrode at different current rates are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0003">3c</a>. The specific discharge capacity decreased gradually (121.0, 116.5, 114.4, 112.3, 109.6, and 104.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) with the increasing current rate (from 0.1 to 5 C), but the voltage drop remained relatively small even at 5C, indicating its potential for fast charging-discharging capabilities.</p>
            
            <p>The rate performances of the four samples are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0003">3d</a>. At a low current density (0.1 C), the specific discharge capacities of single-element-doped samples (NFPP-Mn and NFPP-F) were lower than those of the bare sample (NFPP) and the co-doped sample (NFPP-Mn-F). However, as the current density increased (from 0.1 C to 5 C), the benefits of the specific discharge capacity of the doped samples gradually became apparent. At a current density of 5C, the difference in the specific discharge capacities of the four cathodes reached a maximum. Specifically, the specific discharge capacities of the NFPP, NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F, and NFPP-Mn-F cathodes were 75.7, 96.8, 90.6, and 104.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Notably, for the NFPP-Mn-F cathode, the capacity retention was higher than that of the other three samples across all tested current densities, which demonstrates that suitable doping strategies play a positive role in improving the rate performance of NFPP. The long-term cycling stabilities of the four samples were evaluated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0003">3e</a> (1 C, 1.8-4.2&nbsp;V, 30 °C). The NFPP and NFPP-F samples cycled for 213 and 607 times, respectively, until their capacity retention dropped to 80%. After 1000 cycles at 1C, the capacity retentions of the NFPP, NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F, and NFPP-Mn-F cathodes were 13.0%, 85.6%, 12.7%, and 88.5% respectively. As a result, all three doping strategies (Mn<sup>2+</sup> or F<sup>−</sup> doping, or dual-doping) can significantly improve the cycling stability of NFPP material, especially Mn/F dual-doping. The possible reasons include the enhanced diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> demonstrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0001">1</a> and the medium spin Fe<sup>2+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0002">2</a>). Half-cell tests are not sufficient to demonstrate the feasibility of the material in practical applications. As a result, a full cell was assembled using commercial hard carbon as the anode and NFPP-Mn-F materials as the cathode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). Based on Na metal as a counter electrode, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14a</a> (Supporting Information) showed the cycling performance of commercial hard carbon with a capacity retention of 36.6% after 100 cycles at 1000 mAg<sup>−1</sup>. The charge-discharge profiles of full cell and hard carbon are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14b</a> (Supporting Information). The specific discharge capacity of NFPP-Mn-F electrode reached 107.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C. The specific charge capacity of commercial hard carbon reached 291.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mAg<sup>−1</sup>. After 100 cycles at 1C, the capacity retention and the energy density retention of NFPP-Mn-F cathode were 78.8% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14c</a>, Supporting Information) and 76.2% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14d</a>, Supporting Information), respectively. To further elucidate the phenomena observed, additional tests will be conducted later.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202309701-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202309701-sec-0050-title">2.3 Na<sup>+</sup> Storage Mechanism and Na<sup>+</sup> Dynamics</h3>
            
            <p>The structural evolution of the NFPP and dual-doping NFPP-Mn-F electrodes during the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> was studied through ex situ XRD patterns taken at varying potentials during the first cycle. As seen in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0004">4a</a>, the contour maps from the ex situ XRD test of the NFPP electrode, which correspond to the charge/discharge curves in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), show how peaks such as (011) and (210) shifted to higher 2<i>θ</i> values during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction process and then reverted to their initial states during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion process. This confirms that only a single-phase structural evolution takes place during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction in the NFPP electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0002_R_d126472933e1412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Carbon Energy">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0005_R_d126472933e1412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Changes in the lattice parameters and volume during the first cycle are depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a</a> (Supporting Information) and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0004">4b</a> respectively, as calculated from peaks in the ex situ XRD test. The lattice diminished during the charging process, after which a reversible lattice change was noted during the discharge process, correlating with a volume change of ≈4.38%. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0004">4c</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0004">4d</a> illustrate the structural evolution of the dual-doping NFPP-Mn-F electrodes during the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>. The results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0004">4d</a>) show that after Mn/F dual-doping, only about a 2.74% volume change was observed during the charging process, likely explaining the excellent cyclic stability achieved after Mn/F dual-doping.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202309701-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2d36a769-9a13-4a1f-b0ea-5c3800d5f0e2/adfm202309701-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2d36a769-9a13-4a1f-b0ea-5c3800d5f0e2/adfm202309701-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8d502bcd-56d9-4265-96a7-1d40966d7702/adfm202309701-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2d36a769-9a13-4a1f-b0ea-5c3800d5f0e2/adfm202309701-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202309701-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202309701" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodium storage mechanism and Na<sup>+</sup> dynamics. Corresponding contour maps of ex situ XRD tests collected in the 1st cycle of a) NFPP and c) NFPP-Mn-F cathodes. Volume changes produced by the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> of b) NFPP and d) NFPP-Mn-F. e) Diffusion coefficients of Na<sup>+</sup>. The black, blue, green, and red circles indicate the values of diffusion coefficients of Na<sup>+</sup> for NFPP, NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F, and NFPP-Mn-F cathodes, respectively. The inserted four horizontal lines represent the average value of the entire charge-discharge process. f) The charge/discharge differential capacity versus voltage profiles (<i>dQ</i>/<i>dV</i>) during 200 cycling process of NFPP-Mn-F. g) Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy spectra of the four electrodes after the 1st, 100th, 200th, and 300th cycles.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique was employed to determine Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0027_R_d126472933e1473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Small Struct Energy Storage Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> during the first initial charge-discharge cycle for four as-prepared four samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0004">4e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients during the extraction (or insertion) from (or into) the NFPP frameworks ranged from 10<sup>−10</sup> to 10<sup>−8</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> within the voltage window of 1.8–4.2&nbsp;V. Notably, after Mn, F, or Mn/F doping, the Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion coefficients for NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F, and NFPP-Mn-F electrodes all showed similar improvements. Among them, the Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion coefficients (average value during charge–discharge process) of the NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F, and NFPP-Mn-F electrodes were 3.39E10<sup>−10</sup>, 1.70E10<sup>−10</sup>, and 2.40E10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, exhibiting significant improvement relative to the NFPP sample (1.74E10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>). This is also reflected in the rate performance of the electrodes, further indicating that dual-doping improves the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient and the rate performance of the cathodes.</p>
            
            <p>As the number of charge and discharge cycles increase, problems that may not surface during the first cycle will gradually become evident. To further evaluate the phase transformation of the four as-prepared samples, the differential capacity versus voltage profiles (<i>dQ/dV</i>) of the first two hundred cycles were closely examined (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0004">4f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The 4th cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0004">4f</a>, black line) revealed two pairs of well-defined redox peaks and two pairs of weak redox peaks, representing the Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> redox couple during sodium ion insertion/extraction processes in different sites,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0028_R_d126472933e1538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Power Sources J. Power Sources">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> consistent with Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0002">2b</a>. The difference between the neighboring redox peak and reduction peak, known as Δ<sub>x</sub> (<i>x</i> = 1−4), indicates electrode polarization.</p>
            
            <p>For the NFPP sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>, Supporting Information), Δ-values increased after 200 cycles and the peak strength sharply decreased, indicating increased electrode polarization and significantly decreased specific capacity. The NFPP-Mn sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a>, Supporting Information) showed an increase in Δ-values from 0.2979 (Δ<sub>1</sub>) to 0.6287&nbsp;V (Δ<sub>2</sub>), and from 0.2667 (Δ<sub>3</sub>) to 0.5611&nbsp;V (Δ<sub>4</sub>), with a not-so-serious decrease in peak strength. For the NFPP-F sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9c</a>, Supporting Information), Δ-values increased from 0.2536 (Δ<sub>1</sub>) to 0.6814&nbsp;V (Δ<sub>2</sub>), and from 0.2192 (Δ<sub>3</sub>) to 0.6191&nbsp;V (Δ<sub>4</sub>), with a serious decrease in peak strength. For the NFPP-Mn-F electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0004">4f</a>), Δ<sub>1</sub> increased from 0.1962 to 0.3128&nbsp;V (Δ<sub>2</sub>) after 200 cycles, while Δ<sub>3</sub> increased from 0.1398 to 0.2731&nbsp;V (Δ<sub>4</sub>). Compared to the other three cathodes, the initial Δ-value of the NFPP-Mn-F electrode was relatively small, indicating low polarization and smaller overall electrochemical reaction resistance. After 200 cycles, the Δ-value of the NFPP-Mn-F electrode increased the least among the four samples, and more importantly, the peak strength of the NFPP-Mn-F electrode hardly decreased, representing excellent cycling performance due to low internal resistance and superb structural stability.</p>
            
            <p>To further investigate the reaction kinetics of the four as-prepared samples, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy spectra were collected during the first 300 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0004">4g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). The equivalent circuit model (inset in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0004">4g</a>) was used to analyze the impedance spectra, where the charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) is related to the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> between the cathode and electrolyte<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0029_R_d126472933e1610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">29</a>-<a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0031_R_d126472933e1610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Small Methods">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The calculated impedance parameters are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information). It is noteworthy that the Mn/F dual-doping NFPP-Mn-F cathode exhibited the lowest Rct value (1251 Ω) among the four as-prepared samples (the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of NFPP, NFPP-Mn, and NFPP-F are 3943; 1290, and 2290 Ω, respectively) after 100 cycles. This is beneficial for reducing kinetic restrictions. The Rct values for all four cathodes gradually increased with cycling. Furthermore, the Rct values for NFPP-Mn-F at various cycles were consistently the lowest among the four samples, further confirming enhanced charge transfer in the Mn/F dual-doping NFPP-Mn-F cathode, consistent with the conclusion of long cycling in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0003">3</a>.</p>
            
            <p>To elucidate the critical role of Mn/F doping in boosting the electrochemical performance of NFPP cathodes for sodium storage, DFT calculations were conducted (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0005">5</a>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11, S12</a>, and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). For simplification, the simulations used a bare NFPP with no carbon layer to assess the impact of element doping on charge distribution. As revealed by Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0005">5a,b</a>, and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information), the charge density differences of NFPP, NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F, and NFPP-Mn-F were calculated. The results revealed an imbalanced charge distribution in the four as-prepared samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0005">5a</a>, and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information), with significant charge transfer occurring after Mn, F, or Mn/F dual-doping (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0005">5b</a>). Subsequently, the bandgaps of the four as-prepared samples were substantially altered.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202309701-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202309701-bib-0032_R_d126472933e1654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nano Energy">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0005">5c–f</a>, the bandgaps of NFPP, NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F, and NFPP-Mn-F were 3.4172, 2.6209, 0.3529, and 0.2084&nbsp;eV, respectively. This implies that element doping effectively reduced the NFPP's bandgap, especially in the NFPP-Mn-F sample, thereby enhancing the material's electronic conductivity, which is advantageous for the electrochemical performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202309701-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bbd9feea-c3e8-44ad-bc45-4432267a68e5/adfm202309701-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/bbd9feea-c3e8-44ad-bc45-4432267a68e5/adfm202309701-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cfd14f60-0695-4fa8-89bd-c654dc69cfef/adfm202309701-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bbd9feea-c3e8-44ad-bc45-4432267a68e5/adfm202309701-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202309701-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202309701" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">DFT calculation. a,b) Charge density difference and projected density of states (PDOS) for c) NFPP, d) NFPP-Mn, e) NFPP-F, and f) NFPP-Mn-F.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The magnetic moments for each system are indicated, and the numerical values of NFPP, NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F, and NFPP-Mn-F were 48, 43, 39, and 40 µB, respectively. In a high spin state, the spin directions of two unpaired electrons on each orbit are opposite, canceling each other out, which results in a decrease in the total magnetic moment of the material. In low spin states, the spin direction of unpaired electrons in each orbit is independent of the spin direction of other electrons, yielding a stronger total magnetic moment of the material. Therefore, compared to the high-spin NFPP-F (with the smallest magnetic moment among the four samples), low-spin NFPP (with the largest magnetic moment among the four samples), and NFPP-Mn, the NFPP-Mn-F sample displays a medium spin. This is in agreement with the earlier conclusion in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202309701-fig-0002">2</a>, indicating a balance of neither too weak nor too strong bond strength between the oxidation and reduction processes, hence contributing to a competitive rate performance.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202309701-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202309701-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>NASICON-type Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> materials and different element-doped (Mn, F, and Mn/F dual-doped) particles have been successfully synthesized via a facile one-step spray drying method. As cathodes, the Mn/F dual-doping NFPP-Mn-F electrodes exhibited excellent rate performances of 121.0 and 104.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and 5 C, respectively. Furthermore, they showcased impressive cycling stability (with 88.5% capacity retention after 1000 cycles at 1C) and achieved almost 100% ICE.</p>
         
         <p>Both XRD and TEM results confirmed the expanded interplanar spacing, which enhances the kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> during cycling. Tests including XANES, M-T, and EPR unraveled the local electronic state and electron spin of the as-prepared samples, revealing a medium-spin of Fe in the NFPP-Mn-F sample. This spin leads to a moderate bond strength between the oxidation and reduction processes. Further, UV−vis DRS and DFT calculations proved that Mn/F dual-doping can effectively reduce the band gap of the material. The four-point probe conductivity measurement affirmed that the electron conductivity of the material has been improved, thereby enhancing the electrochemical performance. These findings suggest that the Mn/F dual-doping strategy can effectively improve the electrochemical performance of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> cathodes. More exhaustive investigations into its real application in large-scale energy storage systems are expected in the near future.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202309701-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202309701-sec-0070-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p>All the reagents used in the synthesis were analytical grade and purchased from the Sinopharm. The Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> material was synthesized by a simple spray drying method with a subsequent high-temperature annealing. Commonly, 0.06&nbsp;mol NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O, 0.06&nbsp;mol C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>8</sub>O<sub>7</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O, and 0.045&nbsp;mol Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O were dissolved in 90&nbsp;mL deionized water under intense stirring until a pale yellow-green solution was obtained. After that, the above solution was spray-dried at 260 °C to get a yellow powder precursor. Finally, the as-prepared sample was annealed at 300 °C for 5&nbsp;h and after 550 °C for 8&nbsp;h under an Ar-H<sub>2</sub> (92/8 vol%) atmosphere with a heating rate of 5 °C to obtain the final carbon decorated Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (denoted as NFPP). In addition, the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3-x</sub>Mn<sub>x</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>F<sub>y</sub> and Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3-x</sub>Mn<sub>x</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>F<sub>y</sub> samples (denoted as NFPP-Mn, NFPP-F and NFPP-Mn-F, respectively) were synthesized in the same process, except some of the Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O was replaced with (CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>2</sub>Mn·4H<sub>2</sub>O, or added a certain amount of extra NaF. As the specific doping amounts involve the company's trade secret (Sichuan Enatrium New Energy Sci-Tech Co. Ltd), they are not listed here. If readers are interested in these parameters, please consult the corresponding authors.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202309701-sec-0080-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
         
         <p>Y.X., X.W., and H.W. contributed equally to this work. This work was supported by Natural Science Foundation of Shaanxi (2020JC-41), Natural Science Foundation of Shaanxi (2021TD-15), Scientific Research Program Funded by the Education Department of Shaanxi Provincial Government, and the National Natural Science Foundation of China (52072298, 51802261 and 22172117), the Local Special Service Program Funded by Education Department of Shananxi Provincial Government (19JC031).
